Question No 38 by Clive Needle (H−0844/96)

Subject: Evaluation of aid in Bosnia‐Herzegovina

What evaluation has the Council undertaken to date to the effectiveness of EU aid for reconstruction and for refugee assistance in Bosnia‐Herzegovina, and what assessment does it make of the analysis of any such evaluation?

Answer

In answer to his question on evaluation of aid in Bosnia‐Herzegovina, I would like to draw the attention of the honourable Member of the European Parliament to the General Affairs Council conclusions concerning exYugoslavia of 1 October 1996. At this occasion the Council took note of the information provided by the Commission on the current status of implementation of the European Union assistance programmes for reconstruction in Bosnia‐Herzegovina.

After a debate and considering also the useful comments made by the High Representative on the occasion of the informal ministerial meeting in September, the Council ‚invited the Commission to accelerate the implementation of these programmes while recalling that the conclusions agreed at its meeting of 30 October 1995 remain fully applicable.’ The Council was also in a position to note ‚that the Commission will put forward the necessary proposals aiming to enhance the coherence of the procedures in order to respond adequately to the situation on the ground.’

Moreover, at the same meeting the Council ‚reiterated the importance it attaches to ensuring continuing high priority for concrete measures promoting the return of refugees and displaced persons’ and noted that since July 1996 ‚the Community has committed appropriations to a total of 50 Mecu to facilitate the return of refugees and displaced persons and noted that these projects would serve to promote the return of refugees to the areas most affected by the conflict.’

In this context it is also useful to note that the first meeting of the committee established under Article 12 of Council Regulation 1628/96 relating to aid for former Yugoslavia took place in September, establishing thus the necessary forum for consultations regarding aid for reconstruction. At the same time efforts are also being made to improve coordination between aid allocated through this Council Regulation 1628/96, the PHARE programme, and aid allocated through ECHO for humanitarian purposes, while respecting the procedures of the relevant legal instruments. It may also be noted that the Commission has made efforts to improve the coordination of the European Union aid aimed at promoting return of refugees.

It should finally be underlined that the Council will continue to assess the effectiveness of the European Union aid in Bosnia‐Herzegovina with a view to ensuring the best possible results that can be achieved under the circumstances. For this purpose, the Council intends to focus the European Union aid programmes on the promotion of regional approach which has been particularly developed as the basis for European Union policies with regard to non‐associated countries in South‐Eastern Europe.

Question No 39 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0654/96)

Subject: Democracy and respect for human rights in Albania

On 20 July 1996 the European Parliament adopted a resolution immediately after the ‚elections’ in Albania calling on the Commission and the Council to suspend any further cooperation between the European Union and Albania until respect for democracy and human rights was restored in that country and fresh elections were held in accordance with international democratic standards. The OSCE has also adopted similar resolutions. What measures has the Council taken – or does it intend to take – forthwith to implement the resolution adopted by the democratically elected Parliament of the European Union? How has the Albanian Government reacted?

Answer

The Council has publicly deplored the irregularities which occurred during the electoral process in Albania. In their declarations the Council and the presidency urged all parties to contribute constructively to the accomplishment of the electoral process in conformity with international democratic standards. Hence the Council has suspended the process to conclude with Albania a new agreement which would be an important step towards an association agreement.

The Council has also exhorted all parties to show the highest degree of responsibility and to resume the dialogue with the aim of consolidating the rule of law and democracy in Albania.

The Council has made it clear that a positive stance from the Albanian Government is considered an essential element for the further fruitful development of relations between the European Union and Albania. The Council will closely follow the process of the local elections in Albania to be held in October.

Question No 40 by Katerina Daskalaki (H−0674/96)

Subject: Assassination of Greek Cypriots

What measures will the Council take following the shameful assassination of two Greek Cypriots, 24 year‐old Tassos Isaac and 26‐year‐old Solomos Solomou, who were murdered on 11 and 14 August 1996 respectively by Turkish Cypriot soldiers and members of the ‚Grey Wolves’ terrorist organization (who were transferred to Cyprus at the initiative of the Turkish Foreign Affairs Ministry, as revealed in the Turkish parliament), and who also wounded tens of other Greek Cypriots, as well as members of the UN peace‐keeping force?

Following the statements made by the Turkish Foreign Minister, Mrs Tansu Çiller, who drew a complete veil over the assassinations, what position will the Council adopt, particularly as regards the EU‐Turkey customs union?

Answer

The declaration by the presidency on behalf of the European Union on 16 August clearly demonstrates the Council's position by strongly deploring the recent violence in Cyprus and the disproportionate use of force by the security forces in the north of Cyprus in response to unauthorized entry into the buffer zones. It condemned the brutal killings of Tassos Isaac and Solomos Solomou and expressed deep concern about the injuries sustained by many others, including United Nations peace‐keeping personnel. The Union called for restraint and calm in the wake of these tragic events and urged both sides to avoid further confrontation and to cooperate fully with UNFICYP in its efforts to defuse tensions.

These tragic events have again highlighted the urgent need to intensify efforts to achieve a comprehensive political settlement in Cyprus, under the aegis of the United Nations. The Council reiterated its full support for the work of the Secretary‐General and his special representative in this regard.

Following the recent death of Petros Kakoullis the presidency on behalf of the European Union made another declaration on 16 October condemning this killing and reiterating the points made in the 16 August statement, including the need for restraint. It also called on both sides to look urgently at measures aimed at removing live ammunition and demanning.

As far as EU‐Turkey relations are concerned, the Council has recently recalled in its declaration on Turkey its attachment to the development of good neighbourly relations among all countries of the region. In this context, the Council recalled, inter alia, that relations between Turkey and the EU had to be based on a clear commitment to the principle of respect for international law and agreements. Furthermore, in accordance with the request of the EP when it gave its assent to the Customs Union, the Council is monitoring the human rights situation and democratic development in Turkey.

II. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 44 by Mark Killilea (H−0806/96)

Subject: Liberalization of air transport

Following the liberalization of air transport in the European Union, what impact has this had on reducing air fares, boosting passenger numbers, improving connections between peripheral regions and facilitating tourism growth and off‐peak travel for business and leisure reasons?

Answer

The liberalization of air transport since 1993 has led to some reduction in air fares – particularly on routes where competition is most marked because more than two airlines are operating.

The examples of that include routes such as Barcelona/Madrid, UK/Ireland, Paris/London, some domestic journeys in Germany, in France and in the UK and from Belgium to various European destinations. On other routes it is a matter of regret that the level of so‐called ‚fully flexible’ fares has remained high. It is worth noting, however, that about 71 % of passengers on scheduled flights benefit from reduced fares as a result of Pex, Apex and special offer schemes and between 50 and 55 % of all passengers are on charter flights. As a result, something between 90 and 95 % of customers have the advantage of relatively moderate fares.

At the beginning of the third package of civil aviation liberalization there were 490 routes; now there are approximately 520 – a contrast with experience in the US where the number of routes fell substantially as a result of liberalization. In the Community 30 % of routes are served by two operators and 7 % by three operators or more, a rise from 2 % in 1993. Most of the 64 % of routes still operated as monopolies have low levels of traffic and are therefore not attractive to other carriers. Furthermore, some routes experience real competition from neighbouring routes, from charter services or from other modes of transport such as high speed trains.

The opportunities of access to the market have been increasingly used: on many domestic routes new operators have entered the market; there are now 30 routes operated on a 5th freedom basis by comparison with just 14 in January 1993; and routes operated with cabotage traffic have increased from nil in 1993 to 20 today.

In the three years since 1993 80 new airlines have been created but 60 have ceased to trade. The proportion which continue to operate is significantly higher than that experienced in the early years of liberalization in the USA. Meanwhile charter companies have also increased competition on regular routes.

A thorough analysis of the impact of the Third Package is available in the report adopted today by the Commission. This report will naturally be deposited with this Parliament.

***

Question No 45 by Alan Donnelly (H−0726/96)

Subject: Italy – discriminatory taxation of cars over 2 litres

It has been brought to the Commission's attention repeatedly over the past 18 months that vehicles in Italy with engines over 2l are subject to unfavourable tax treatment as compared to vehicles under 2l. Considering that the vast majority of vehicles over 2l are imported from other Member States, does this tax treatment not constitute a discrimination contrary to Articles 30 and 95 of the Treaty of Rome? Why has the Commission allowed 18 months to pass without taking action?

Answer

Several European car producers complained to the European Commission about the Italian taxation system for cars above two litres; namely the ‚imposta straordinaria’ and the corporation tax. According to them, both taxes have a discriminatory effect, as they tax more heavily cars above the threshold and limit certain fiscal advantages to cars under two litres.

The Commission has carefully examined the aforementioned taxes with reference to the Treaty provisions on the free movement of goods.

In order to obtain a rapid solution to these problems, the Commission participated in meetings with the Italian authorities.

These meetings have proved particularly fruitful as the Commission has been able to rapidly obtain the repeal of the ‚imposta straordinaria’ that the European car producers described as the ‚major obstacle which they had to face when exporting to Italy’. This repeal brought about an increase in the size of this market by almost 50 %.

However, as the obstacles underlined by the complainants have not been completely eliminated, a further meeting with the Italian authorities has taken place.

In this framework, it is worth noting that the new draft budget law provides a modification of the corporation tax which seems to reduce, albeit not eliminating, the alleged discrimination.

The Commission will shortly hold a meeting with the Italian tax authorities to discuss the effects of the draft budget law and to foster the complete elimination of the remaining obstacles.

***

Question No 50 by Marianne Eriksson (H−0788/96)

Subject: Financing of priority TENs projects

At September's Ecofin meeting, the Council said no to financing for the rail projects included amongst the 14 top‐priority TENs projects.

What steps will the Commission take to ensure that these projects are implemented?

Answer

The honourable Member has taken the view that the Ecofin recently ‚said no to financing for the rail projects included amongst the 14 top‐priority TENs projects’ as she says in her question, since some of the reporting of that Council has inadvertently been misleading. A brief background is therefore in order.

In response to the calls for proposals for a ‚top‐up’ for the TENs budget made by the European Council since its Essen meeting, and using figures provided by the Member States, the Commission estimated that additional financing needs for the 14 priority projects amounted to ECU 1.7 billion. Recognizing the general budgetary constraints, however, the Commission then requested an extra ECU 1 billion as leverage finance. It is this sum which has been refused by the Ecofin, not because of doubts about whether the extra spending was worthwhile, but because of the need which they felt to ‚repatriate’ available resources. The Commission notes that decision – but naturally remains convinced that the allocation of such a top‐up would have helped to generate jobs, improve competitiveness and protect the environment with relevant studies and through long‐term progress with decongestion.

The regrettable decision by Ecofin does not, of course, diminish the importance of TENs and the Commission, with the other institutions, will continue to work for the development of these essential networks.

The ECU 1.8 billion which has been programmed for the Community's trans‐European transport network budget in the period 1995‐1999 will therefore continue to be spent effectively and the major part of these funds will still be targeted on the 14 priority projects, of which 8 are rail and 2 combined rail/road projects. Whilst a ‚top‐up’ would have been a very useful means of broadening and accelerating work on valuable projects, therefore, substantial progress is still possible and will be achieved.

The EIB has granted loans for 9 of the 14 priority projects (or their initial phases) amounting to ECU 5.2bn, and is giving consideration to loans for the other five projects. The EIF is also involved in financing two of the priority projects. In addition two of the rail priority projects (HST South Spain‐France and the Irish rail projects) are receiving significant assistance from structural funds.

Whilst the Community TEN budget is very valuable for leveraging investment, especially in the earliest stages of projects, the main responsibility for financing the projects, as the House is well aware, remains with Member States. The fact that they will gain – individually and collectively – from the completion of the network as a whole will, motivate them to attach a high national priority in the provision of funds to the transport priority projects.

The honourable Member might also be interested to know that a high‐level group of representatives from Member States and the private sector has been established to study the possibilities for establishing public/private funding and development partnerships for transport projects and that group is giving particular attention to the rail sector.

Development of the networks is, of course, not only a matter of funding new infrastructure, it is also a matter of improving the use of existing infrastructure. The Commission initiative to establish European Rail Freight Freeways and research work into transport telematics are just two examples of the way in which network development can be promoted without large expenditure on new physical infrastructure.

As a result of misinterpretation of the outcome of the recent Ecofin, it has been said that ‚TENs is dead’. The Commission is therefore very pleased to have this opportunity to disprove that and to adapt the words of Mark Twain – ’reports of TENs death are greatly exaggerated...’

***

Question No 51 by David Bowe (H−0843/96)

Subject: Transport

Will the Commission consider the introduction of a regulation making the installation of air‐bags in passenger seats in aircraft mandatory within the European Union, and if not, why not?

Answer

Although the protection of passengers in case of accident is largely ensured by the use of seat belts, it is recognized that protection against head injuries should be improved for certain seats in the aircraft, particularly these behind bulkheads or with a door in front.

Research is going on to reach this objective and the use of airbags is one of the possible solutions. Nevertheless, there is insufficient technical information to privilege this option.

For this reason the Commission is not at present planning to introduce a regulation on this matter, but is introducing into Community law an amendment to the presently applicable rules which would ensure that new generation aircraft provide sufficient protection against head injuries for all passengers in case of crash.

Question No 56 by Veronica Hardstaff (H−0760/96)

Subject: Training in the fishing industry

Does the Commission agree that better training for the fishing industry could contribute to solving some of the problems currently being experienced within the common fisheries policy due to bad practices and insufficient awareness of the need for conservation measures? Are any measures in place throughout the Union to coordinate training in this important industry organized on a Community wide basis? If not, could consideration be given to addressing this possibility?

Answer

The Commission agrees with the honourable Member that the training of fishermen must certainly take into account the conservation of stocks and the common fisheries policy in general.

At Community level, several steps have been taken in this respect. There are two that can be mentioned in the context of the Structural Funds:

‐ Among the guidelines laid down by the Commission in 1994 for the PESCA Community Initiative Programme, express mention was made of ‚training in biological and economic fisheries management and in the common fisheries policy’. The majority of the Member States have adopted this measure in their programmes.

‐ Various operational programmes connected with Objective 1 include the vocational training or retaining of fishermen.

Question No 57 by Bertel Haarder (H−0766/96)

Subject: Implementation of the Toys Directive

The objective of the Toys Directive (88/378/EEC)(3) is to approximate national safety standards for toys to ensure that children are not injured while at play. The Danish consumer authority has told an individual member of the public that the use of solid glass hopscotch ‚stones’ has been prohibited, under European Standard DSEN 71‐1 drawn up on the basis of that directive. Danish children have played hopscotch with solid glass stones for centuries.

Can the Commission confirm that the Toys Directive does not require any ban on solid Danish glass hopscotch stones, and that if any such ban exists, it is the transposition of that directive into Danish law which is at fault?

Answer

It would appear that only Danish children play this European game with a glass disc, known in Danish as a ‚glashinkesten’ (pronunciation = {glass‐hyn‐que‐stéén}), which explains why this article and its name exist only in Danish.

Neither the Directive nor its transposition into Danish law prohibits the use of this glass disc. The legislation imposes no restrictions or obligations on children. It targets the production and marketing of toys. This Directive does not prohibit the ‚glashinkesten’, provided it complies with the essential safety requirements laid down in this Directive.

Since the Directive became applicable in January 1990, a toy may no longer be marketed without bearing the mark ‚CE’, which, in accordance with the procedures set out below, certifies that it conforms to the essential requirements of the Directive. The producer can choose between two different ways of arranging to obtain this mark. He can follow the harmonized standard mentioned in the question, or he can submit a model of his toy to an approved body which will issue him with a standard CE certificate if, on examination, the model is found to comply with the essential requirements of the Directive.

It is true that Standard EN 71‐1 prohibits the use of glass in the manufacture of toys, except in cases where the glass is necessary for the function of the toy, as for example in the case of optical toys.

The Consumer Directorate in Denmark confirmed on 27 September 1996 that it has never been called upon to reach any decision regarding the conformity of these glass discs with the Directive, since no consumer or producer has approached it on this matter. In other words, no one has ever considered whether this toy technically complies with the Directive.

It is therefore impossible to say whether such a certificate could be obtained.

***

Question No 58 by Clive Needle (H−0767/96)

Subject: Effects of dredging and mineral extraction in coastal waters

What actions can the Commission take to help and protect fish and shellfish industries and communities which suffer from the effects of dredging and mineral extraction in coastal waters, such as currently affects the Wash in eastern England?

Answer

The Commission is informed that inshore fishermen operating in the Wash estuary on the east coast of England are experiencing declining catches of cockles and shrimp mainly.

The reason for this has been attributed by fishermen to the dredging for sand in the area but this conclusion is disputed. A monitoring programme has been commenced by the UK authorities in an attempt to establish the actual position more accurately.

According to Directive 85/337/EEC, on the assessment of the effects of certain public and private projects on the environment, proposals for the extraction of minerals are to be subject to environmental assessment only when Member States consider that their characteristics are like to have significant effects on the environment.

In these cases Member States authorities are required to follow certain procedures for publicising information. The purpose of these procedures is to protect the environment. As long as Member States have followed such procedures, the decision whether or not to proceed with the extraction is entirely within their competence.

King's Lynn and Boston are fishing ports located in the region of the Wash. Both of these qualify as areas dependent on fishing. Therefore they are eligible for financial aid under the UK/PESCA Programme. Consequently, Member States authorities can support measures intended to counteract the effects of a decline in fishing, providing these are eligible under the various financial instruments and funds involved in the programme.

Question No 59 by Bryan Cassidy (H−0769/96)

Subject: Labelling of cosmetic products

In Official Journal C 265 of 12 September 1996, the European Commission published open invitation to tender No 96/C 265/05 for a survey to be conducted aimed at ‚inter‐alia’, current consumer requirements regarding the labelling of foodstuffs:

Identifying and drawing up an inventory of the pieces of information which are possibly of no use to the majority of consumers... distinguishing those concerning a majority of consumers from those which are only of interest to specific consumer categories.

Identifying means of information which substitute classical labelling and which would be acceptable to consumers.

Will the Commission follow up this very praiseworthy initiative on food labelling with a similar one on the labelling of cosmetic products?

Answer

As regards cosmetic products' labelling, the Commission does not envisage publishing an open invitation to tender similar to that published in OJ C 265 of 12 September 1996 under number 96/C2265/05 regarding the labelling of foodstuffs.

The reason for that position is the fact that Council Directive 93/35/EEC of 14 June 1993, amending for the sixth time Directive 76/768/EEC on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to cosmetic products, which has to be implemented after 1.1.1997, already provides for completion of the labelling of cosmetic products to ensure better information for the consumer.

In addition to the previous classical indications, which were the identity of the manufacturer, the nominal content, the date of minimum durability, the particular precautions to be observed in use and the batch number of manufacture, the following indications have been added; the function of the product and, above all, the list of ingredients in descending order of weight.

This is very important information for the consumer who, even if he is not a chemist, can in any case be given advice by his doctor, eg in cases of personal allergy to a substance. In this case the consumer will have the possibility to avoid buying cosmetic products containing this specific substance.

In this context the Commission adopted on 8 May 1996 a common nomenclature (OJEC L 132 of 1 June 1996) which should make it possible for the consumer to recognize through the Community and, in fact, in many countries all over the world, a substance which he would like to avoid using.

Question No 60 by Robin Teverson (H−0783/96)

Subject: Youth hostels in the European Union

Following the tragic and unlawful death of my constituent, Caroline Dickinson, in July at a youth hostel in Brittany, France, while staying there with other members from her school, what plans does the Commission have to ensure that parents can rely on minimum standards of security and safety when their children stay in hostel accommodation throughout the European Union?

Answer

The quality and safety of services such as youth hostels, as well as the associated theme liability of the service providers in case of problems, are vast issues which the Commission has been considering for some time. A proposal for a directive on service liability was, as you know, put on the table by the Commission in 1990 but had to be withdrawn four years later due to vehement opposition from many quarters. However, the industry itself operates safety standards. Hostels affiliated with the International Youth Hostel Federation, including the hostels of the European Community Federation of Youth Hostels Associations, have to respect certain safety standards.

As far as the tragic incident referred to by the honourable Member is concerned, the Commission has been informed that the attack was made possible by the necessity for the hostel to stay open at night and hence accessible, in order to respect local fire safety regulations. This is of course disturbing, but you will agree it is not for the Commission to resolve such local difficulties.

Question No 61 by Bernd Posselt (H−0785/96)

Subject: Humanitarian aid in the Caucasus

After the unrest and electoral irregularities in Armenia, and in view of the further deterioration of the situation in Chechnya, the problem of refugees and displaced persons in the Caucasus is likely to get worse. What is the current situation with regard to humanitarian aid for the whole troubled region of the Caucasus?

Answer

The situation in the Caucasus is, of course, different in the North (Chechnya) where the consequences of the recent conflict are still present, compared to the Transcaucasus (Georgia, Armenia and Azerbaijan) where an acute humanitarian crisis no longer exists.

For the Northern Caucasus, as a result of the heavy fighting that has characterized most of 1996, the population is facing another winter of severe hardship. Since 1994 more than ECU 31m have been spent on budget for first aid, medical aid and logistics in the three republics, Chechnya, Ingushetia and Dagestan. A new allocation of ECU 2.2m is planned before the end of 1996. A Commission mission is visiting the area in order to find out the needs for the beginning of 1997.

The three Southern Caucasus Republics – Armenia, Azerbaijan and Georgia – are in a post‐emergency situation. There are indications that the socio‐economic decline has come to a standstill and that recovery is taking root. In this context, discussions with (institutional) donors, relevant authorities and within the Commission are taking place in order to phase out humanitarian assistance and to find more durable and structural solutions. Structural support by the Commission includes technical assistance through TACIS (ECU 28m during the coming four years for Armenia and ECU 32m each for Azerbaijan and Georgia). Armenia will also benefit from a conditional grant of ECU 13m in support of agricultural sector reform, while Azerbaijan and Georgia will receive ECU 14m and ECU 18m respectively. In each country ECU 1m of technical assistance is included.

In between, humanitarian projects will continue but will be much more accentuated on the most vulnerable. Criteria have been modified to ensure better targeting of food. The variety in food commodities has been reduced and distribution in bulk has increased. In Armenia food assistance has been scaled down. In Azerbaijan the Commission's support to IDPs (Internally Displaced People) in Baku and Sumgait implemented as of 1995 on the request of the Azeri Government, has been phased out, while support to elderly is strengthened. Assistance in Georgia is being directed less towards rural and more towards urban areas.

Question No 62 by Peter Crampton (H−0809/96)

Subject: Third country fishing agreement with Senegal

Could the Commissioner confirm that the zone for artisanal fishermen in Senegal has been re‐established at 12 miles and that the catch allowed for European Union vessels has been reduced, to preserve fish stocks for the future?

Will this now be a general guideline for all future agreements with third countries?

Answer

The negotiations for the conclusions of a new fisheries protocol between the European Community and Senegal are still going on.

A fourth round of negotiations will take place on 31 October and 1 November in Dakar.

Both the European Community and Senegal wish to end the negotiations with a new protocol which will benefit the interests of the European Community and those of Senegal.

As the current negotiations stand at present, Senegal has proposed, and the Community has agreed, for the forthcoming protocols, with regard to inshore demersal wet fishing, vessels in excess of 150 GRT will no longer fish within 12 nautical miles of the shore, instead of 6 nautical miles as is the case at present. This new arrangement will mean that the Senegalese artisanal fishermen are no longer in competition with the Community fleets, thus avoiding any risk of conflict.

Question No 67 by Georges Berthu (H−0818/96)

Subject: Community budget fraud

The press has revealed that the mayor of Sint‐Niklaas (Belgium) is subject to an arrest warrant for misappropriating between BF 10 and 15 million in Community subsidies intended for the association entitled ‚The European Middle Class Union’ of which he is chairman.

Can the Commission state the precise reason why these public funds were disbursed from the Community budget, and why, in an atmosphere of strict budgetary discipline, it was absolutely necessary to do so?

In the 1996 budget, for example, the commentary to the relevant line (B5‐320) refers to:

’contributions to organizations representing the interests of small and medium‐sized enterprises that will enable their members to receive appropriate training and information in the field of European business. ’

In each of these years the funds have been allocated on the basis of a programme of actions proposed by each of the organizations concerned. Funding was not given to subsidize general operating costs.

2. AECM provided the Commission, when requested, with information on its activities, its membership and its office holders.

Subject: Fraud to the detriment of the Commission

In January 1996 the Directorate‐General for Enterprise Policy, Distributive Trades, Tourism and Cooperatives (DG XXIII) found itself at the focus of attention when two of its officials were accused of active corruption in a bribery case.

Question No 70 by Jens‐Peter Bonde (H−0721/96)

Subject: Pesticide residues in milk – product labelling

Will the Commission ensure that the designation ‚milk’ may be used only for products which do not contain pesticide residues or other toxic substances, and will it also ensure that the contents are properly labelled, so that consumers are free to choose?

Answer

The concept of the existence of no pesticide residues in food products is erroneous as residues will be frequently found at very low levels and in many cases below the limits of determination normally employed for control purposes.

Harmonized maximum pesticide residues exist in Council Directive 86/363/EEC(4) on the fixing of maximum pesticide residues in and on foodstuffs of animal origin.

The Commission believes that when milk meets the requirements on composition and quality laid down in Community legislation, there is no ground for refusing the denomination ‚milk’ on selling such products.

Moreover, it should be underlined that consumers, wishing to purchase agriculture products that meet specific conditions regarding their production method, may use organic foodstuffs whose production and sales are covered by Council Regulation (EEC) No 2092/91(5) .

Question No 71 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0723/96)

Subject: Final storage of nuclear fuel

The Act of Accession of Sweden to the EU includes a joint declaration on the application of the Euratom Treaty (Declaration No 4) which states that ‚As regards the back end of the nuclear fuel cycle, it is the responsibility of each Member State to define its own policy’.

Does this declaration imply only that Sweden is entitled to decide on the method for final storage of nuclear fuel or does it mean that Sweden also has a right enshrined in the Act of Accession to refuse to accept waste for final storage from other countries?

What is the legal value of this declaration if, in the future, the EU/Euratom draws up rules governing the final storage of nuclear waste for the Union as a whole?

Answer

In the Commission's view, the paragraph of the Joint Declaration to which the honourable Member refers sets out an interpretation of the Euratom Treaty acknowledging that the Member States are responsible for deciding the policy to follow for the back end of the nuclear fuel cycle. In addition, the Council Resolution of 19 December 1994 (94/C 379/01) states that ‚each Member State is responsible for ensuring that the radioactive waste produced on its territory is properly managed’.

However, the Commission considers that the Joint Declaration does not deal with the question of the refusal by Member States to accept nuclear waste for final storage from other Member States.

While there are no Community legal provisions governing the acceptance of radioactive waste for final storage from other Member States the Directive ‚on the supervision and control of shipments of radioactive waste between Member States and into and out of the Community’ (92/3/EURATOM of 3 February 1992) covers all such waste shipments for whatever purpose. This requires that shipments of radioactive waste between Member States be subject to prior authorization by the Member State of origin. This authorization can only be issued after the Member State of destination has granted its approval.

The Community has so far not obliged a Member State to accept a shipment of radioactive waste from another Member State. The Commission is of the opinion that this practice should also be pursued in the future.

Question No 72 by Rui Vieira (H−0724/96)

Subject: Medicinal plants

The Council Resolution of 20 December 1995(6) on medicinal plant preparations called on the Commission, in cooperation with the Member States, to study the situation that exists with regard to some aspects of the marketing of such products. Given the significance and consumption of these products and the scale of the use of medicinal plants in many countries, does the Commission have available an up‐to‐date list of these plants, has it encouraged and supported the studies and inquires concerning these plants in the various Member States, and what was their response to the need to improve our knowledge of medicinal plants?

Answer

The Commission has no list of plant‐based medicinal products used in the Community. Hitherto, no plant‐based medicinal product has been the subject of one of the new Community marketing authorization procedures. These products are therefore the subject of national marketing authorizations.

The Commission has supported, through the BIOMED programme, the production of 50 monographs on plants for medicinal use, in order to standardize the use of these plant‐based medicinal products in accordance with the common criteria of quality, safety and efficacy.

The competent authorities of Member States have stated that they are available to participate in the study requested by the Council in its Resolution of 20 December 1995.

Question No 73 by Simon Murphy (H−0727/96)

Subject: Commission investigation into IPCL pub leases

Is the Commission aware that delay in the publication of its findings of its investigation into pub leases operated by the Inntrepreneur Pub Company Limited (IPCL) is causing widespread legal uncertainty in the United Kingdom? Question No 74 by Roy Perry (H−0770/96)

Subject: Block exemption regulation for the supply of beer

The delay by the Commission in publishing the findings of its investigation into the pub leases operated by the Inntrepreneur Pub Company Limited (IPCL) is causing legal uncertainty in the entire UK pub leasing business. The uncertainty caused by the Commission's delay has provoked general anxiety in the trade; it has damaged business confidence and is causing increasingly serious disruptive effects on the operations of many UK businesses.

Is the Commission aware of the effect that the delay in publication is having on the UK pub leasing business, and can the Commission say when they will publish their report?

Joint Answer

The Commission is aware that UK brewing circles are eagerly awaiting the outcome of its proceedings with regard to the notification by the Inntrepreneur Pub Company Limited (Inntrepreneur) and the four related complaints of Inntrepreneur tenants.

A crucial part in the Commission's deliberations as to the orientation to give to this case will be the answer to the question of whether tied tenants are put at a disadvantage vis‐à‐vis free tenants. The factual elements on which this question is based are contained in the report of the Office of Fair Trading on their enquiry into UK brewers' wholesale pricing policy of May 1995. However, the Commission only received clearance to use the report in its own proceedings in November 1995. The formal complainants were asked to comment on relevant parts on the report and presented their observations in March 1996. This led to additional fact‐finding by the Commission.

On that basis, the Commission is currently preparing the provisional position which it will take as regards the case.

However, the procedural follow‐up to this provisional position is, to a large extent, beyond the control of the Commission, so that a timing for the definitive decision cannot be given.

Question No 75 by Tony Cunningham (H−0728/96)

Subject: United Kingdom's 1989 Guest Beer Act

Does the Commission accept that the United Kingdom's 1989 Guest Beer Act was drafted and introduced with its approval?

Does the Commission accept that this Act created greater competition and consumer choice in the UK beer market?

Is the Commission aware that prohibition of the current 1989 Guest Beer Act could lead to the closure of over 50 % of the small breweries in the United Kingdom, with significant job losses, and a reduction in consumer choice?

Answer

In his question about the UK's 1989 guest beer law, the honourable Member of the European Parliament refers to the decision of the Commission to issue a reasoned opinion adopted in accordance with the procedure laid down in Article 169 of the EC Treaty.

This procedure has been initiated by a complaint against the requirement that the guest beer should be ‚cask conditioned’. In fact, this condition is discriminatory as it favours a typical national product. According to the information held by the Commission, there is no significant production of ‚cask conditioned’ beer in other Member States which could comply with this criterion. The principle of non‐discrimination between national and Community products is absolutely essential for the internal market to function.

In this legal proceeding, the Commission does not contest the guest beer provision as a whole but only the choice of the ‚cask‐conditioned’ definition (’a draught beer which undergoes fermentation in the container from which it is served for consumption’). In order to comply with Community law, the British authorities should choose further criteria which are not discriminatory.

In 1989 it is true that the Member of the Commission in charge of the competition portfolio, wrote a letter to the UK stating that the draught beer order he had received would not breach the UK's obligations under EC competition law. However, the rules must also be compatible with Community rules on non‐discrimination against products from other Member States and unfair trade barriers (Articles 30‐36).

It is difficult to evaluate whether the law created greater competition and consumer choice in the UK beer market. However, such objectives (as worthy as they may be) do not fall within any of the exceptions to Article 30 contained in Article 36 of the EC Treaty. Regarding the increase of competition, the British‐based Federation of Small Businesses says that the guest beer provision has not worked because large UK brewers are supplying their own ‚cask‐conditioned’ beer as ‚guest beer’.

The Commission does not think that the effect of a change in the law will necessarily lead to the closure of over 50 % of the small breweries in the UK. After all, the ‚cask conditioned’ beers are currently produced by the big breweries and may be sold as ‚guest beers’ to the tenants. In this context, if the United Kingdom authorities chose a turnover or production criterion in order to reserve the right of guest beer to small breweries, this, in turn, could stimulate their production.

***

Question No 76 by Elisabeth Schroedter (H−0730/96)

Subject: Cohesion Fund

The Commission has proposed that the allocation of Structural Fund resources, like Cohesion Fund resources, should be subject to various budgetary conditions geared to monetary union.

Will it stand by its proposal when deciding on the new basis and the objectives of the reform of the Structural Funds?

If so, why?

Answer

The French delegation at the informal Ecofin Council in Verona proposed that the macroeconomic conditions applicable to the Cohesion Fund should be extended to the Structural Funds. This proposal was the subject of a study undertaken by the Commission. This study(7) concludes:

’The purpose of the structural policies, first and foremost, is medium‐ and long‐term development geared to strengthening the sustainable growth potential of problem economies. They thus contribute to the continuity of the associated investment effort. In all the Member States, and more especially in those extensively covered by Objective 1, the Community structural resources have structuring effects on national, regional and local public expenditure. Consequently, the existing apparatus of conditions governing the Cohesion Fund would not take account of the medium‐ and long‐term purpose of the structural funds.

Applying the conditions set up for the Cohesion Fund would mean that all Community structural support for the regions and for the development of human resources would be linked to compliance with a national budgetary discipline.

It could prove difficult to explain to the regions eligible to benefit from the Structural Funds, which in some cases themselves contribute to the budgetary development effort and work to mobilize the regional and local actors, that the community transfers are suspended during the implementation of the programmes for reasons to which the latter are very largely, if not entirely, foreign.

Be that as it may, the penalties associated with the conditions must not be applied to the Structural Funds intended for the co‐financing of regional and local resources.

In any case, conditionality and additionality may not be compatible. It is necessary to encourage nominal convergence, because budgetary reconstruction and monetary stability are necessary conditions for medium‐ and long‐term development. At the same time, however, there can be no question of casting doubt on the process of development of the regions as a condition for sustainable development, for which additionality is a necessary guarantee.

Finally, the programming principle is equivalent to an approach to continuity of the community support, which would be somewhat incompatible with a suspension of financial support.’

Question No 77 by Glyn Ford (H−0731/96)

Subject: Working time directive and sea workers

Given the clear opposition of the UK Government to any restriction on even excessive working time, will the Commission give a date by which working time for ‚other workers at sea’ in the UK will be protected by legislation not dependent on the goodwill of employers or government?

Answer

The Commission is preparing a White Paper on the sectors and activities excluded from the Working Time Directive for publication this Autumn. ‚Other work at sea’ is one of these activities.

The White Paper will contain an analysis of the current situation with regard to each of these sectors and activities and of the options open to the Commission. It will also set out the Commission's views on the way forward on this matter.

The timing of future action will be influenced by many factors, including reactions to the views expressed in the White Paper.

Question No 78 by Arthur Newens (H−0733/96)

Subject: World Trade Organization and rights of representation in trade disputes

In view of the refusal to allow small Caribbean countries fully to participate in the hearing before a panel of the WTO dealing with the dispute over the EU banana import regime as the result of US objections, can the Commission protest at this ruling as it could set a precedent which will give undue influence to one major power?

Answer

The Community has supported the requests advanced by ACP States, including Caribbean States, to participate fully, as interested parties, in the proceedings of the panel established by the World Trade Organization to examine the EC banana regime. The panel has ruled on the issue, allowing for enhanced participation. As a result, third parties have been able to submit written memoranda to the panel, to be present and intervene orally at its first meeting, and to be present and make a statement at the second and final meeting.

The panel has also ruled that only government representatives may appear before the panel.

The Commission wishes to assure the honourable Member that it has been and will continue to be supportive of the ACP States in the context of the proceedings of these panel proceedings.

Question No 80 by Kirsten Jensen (H−0753/96)

Subject: Aid for the production of tomatoes in Italy

How much aid does the EU grant annually for tomato production in Italy and has it led to noticeably higher incomes for Italian tomato growers?

Is the Commission aware of the wages and working conditions of immigrants who harvest tomatoes as described in Berlingske Tidende of 15 September 1996, and does it know whether they conform to the Community standards applicable to wages and social security?

Answer

1. As regards the aid granted to tomato producers, the Community rules (Regulation (EC) No 1035/72) provide an intervention mechanism for products withdrawn from the market. This intervention price is calculated as a function of the basic and purchase prices set by the Council before each marketing season.

Under this system, the Italian producer organizations benefited, for the financial year 1994, to the extent of ECU 0.8 million and ECU 1.3 million in respect of products withdrawn from the market. This represented 17 % and 20 %, respectively, of the total sum earmarked for the withdrawal of tomatoes in the Community.

With regard to the aid system for tomato processing, the processor benefits from Community aid on condition that he pays a minimum price to the producer.

Italy benefited to the extent of ECU 198.6 million in the financial year 1994 (or 55 % of the Community total) and ECU 186.7 million for the financial year 1995 (or 50 % of the Community total), in terms of tomato processing aid.

These measures were instituted in order to guarantee a proper balance between supply and demand and to deal with any fluctuations in the fruit and vegetables market.

2. The principle of equality of treatment laid down by the Community rules on remuneration only benefits, in principle, those workers who are nationals of a Member State. However, this same principle also appears in the agreements concluded with the Maghreb States. Tunisian, Moroccan or Algerian workers who are lawfully employed in a Member State may not, therefore, be discriminated against in the matter of pay or working conditions.

With regard to social security, the Commission would remind the honourable Member that Regulation (EEC) No 1408/71, which coordinates the national social security systems, obliges the Member States to treat migrant workers originating from another Member State in the same way as their own nationals.

However, the entitlement to equal treatment in respect of social security is confined to Community nationals. This is because the Community legislation on the subject does not apply to third country nationals. The fact remains that the entitlement to equal treatment in respect of social security is likewise guaranteed for nationals of Morocco, Algeria and Tunisia, under the cooperation agreements with those countries.

Question No 81 by Anne Van Lancker (H−0754/96)

Subject: Candidacies for examinations at the Commission – age limit

In its resolutions the European Parliament has repeatedly urged that positive measures be adopted to help older workers to find employment.

The Commission too has called for such measures.

As a rule, the Commission operates an age limit of 35 for admission to examinations. Why is this age limit being operated?

Can the Commission take the necessary measures to eliminate this discrimination?

Answer

The Commission, and as a general rule all the other institutions, apply age limits in the external recruitment competitions at basic grades. Annex III of the Staff Regulations provide the institutions with the possibility to specify age limits for admission to competitions.

Article 27 of the Staff Regulations obliges the Commission to ensure that officials are recruited on the broadest geographical basis from among nationals of the Member States. Age limits help to improve the geographical balance of the staff of the institutions. Experience has shown that higher age limits tend to accentuate geographical imbalances since the willingness to expatriate tends to diminish with increasing age. Abolishing age limits would probably lead to an increase in the number of candidates and therefore laureates from the local labour market.

In addition, female participation in competitions at basic grades, where age limits are applied, is much higher than at the more senior grades. The recent enlargement competitions given evidence of this too. Experience suggests that women's geographical mobility tends to diminish more than men's with increasing age and as family obligations are acquired. Therefore, the application of age limits has helped to improve the female/male balance in terms of candidates and laureates.

Since the Commission is a career civil service, age limits are also an instrument in the management of human resources. It is important to have a balanced age structure, offering career perspectives to young officials to progress later into senior positions.

Finally, interinstitutional cooperation in recruitment matters is being reinforced. All the aspects of recruitment policy, including age limits, should in the future be treated within the framework of interinstitutional cooperation.

Subject: Obstruction of exports of Spanish automobile components to France

Is the Commission aware of the obstruction of exports of Spanish automobile components taking place at the French frontier? Does it know that the French authorities are demanding personal sureties from lorry drivers before letting them go? Once it is to hand, this information will be studied to determine whether the measures adopted by the French authorities might be contrary to Articles 30 and 36 of the EC Treaty.

The honourable Member will be kept informed in the event that a complaint is registered and of the results of the inquiry, if any.

Question No 83 by Per Gahrton (H−0759/96)

Subject: Swedish ban on trichlorothylene

Reports in the media suggest that the Commission intends to stop a planned Swedish ban on trichlorothylene, a carcinogenic substance. Sweden notified the ban, due to start in 1997, as long ago as 1991. Businesses have therefore had plenty of time to move over to using other substances which are less harmful to the environment and health. Is it true that the Commission intends to take action to stop the Swedish ban on trichlorothylene? If so, how is such action compatible with the objectives of the EU's environmental programmes and the right enshrined in the Treaty for Member States to maintain environmental protection rules which are more stringent than the EU's own rules?

Answer

The Commission has received complaints from a large number of economic operators regarding a Swedish ban on the industrial use of trichloroethylene and methylene chloride. Exemptions to the ban may be granted. However the economic operators being granted exemptions must pay certain fees.

The Commission's procedure with regard to these complaints is only concerned with the industrial use and not any national rules on consumer use.

Sweden has not in a risk assessment shown sufficient evidence of serious risk posed by the current use of trichloroethylene and methylene chloride in the course of business activities.

There are no restrictions at Community level applying to the marketing and use of trichloroethylene and methylene chloride. The Commission has however (in a declaration for the Council Minutes concerning the 14th amendment to Directive 76/769 on certain chlorinated solvents) expressed its intention to examine before the end of 1997 in consultation with Member States, the case for limitations on the marketing to consumers of these substances.

Although the EC Treaty provides for possibilities for the Member States to derogate from Community law in order to fulfil objectives of public interest, such as public health or the environment, they are not applicable in the case of the Swedish rules. Neither Article 36 or 100a in the Treaty can be applied. The Swedish rules cannot be considered necessary and proportionate in relation to any objective of public interest.

If the industrial use of these products would be considered to cause a risk to the heath of workers which come in contact with them, other measures may be adopted which are less restrictive to intra‐Community trade than the ban and exemption rules. the Commission has suggested that the Swedish authorities lift the ban and exemption rules and introduce controls at work places. If rules on controls at work places are introduced, the measures provided for by the Community legislation on health and safety at work (such as the Council Directives 80/1107/EEC and 89/391/EEC) are to be applied. This legislation lay down minimum requirements under Article 118a which are to be applied in the workplace.

For the complainants, products or methods which are alternatives to trichloroethylene and methylene chloride would cause excessive costs in some cases. In particular, the possibilities to use alternative products or methods are for this reason excluded for the complainants which are small and medium sized companies. Especially pharmaceutical companies have no realistic alternative.

The Commission has decided to send a letter of formal notice to Sweden asking for its observations in this matter.

Question No 84 by José Valverde López (H−0761/96)

Subject: Consumer concern over monitoring and controls in respect of BSE

The BSE epidemic is causing major damage to producers throughout Europe, and is endangering consumers' health and interests. In the face of this grave state of affairs, the Commission, according to its own replies to MEPs' questions, has still not called for an epidemiological study to be launched; it has not expressed an opinion as to the prohibition or otherwise of bonemeal for the feeding of poultry, swine and fish; and it appears to possess no information as to whether veterinary checks on holdings are being carried out or whether the manufacturers of meal from BSE‐infected cattle are being called to account. In view of all this, what is the Commission doing to restore consumer confidence? What guarantees can it offer consumers that EC legislation is being complied with in all the affected sectors? Which industrial sectors were using fats, tallow or gelatine from the UK before the embargo?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the damage caused to the European beef market by consumer concerns about BSE. The Commission has taken all necessary measures to protect the health of consumers ever since the disease was first recognized. In this respect, the Commission would remind the honourable Member that there is still no conclusive evidence that BSE is a danger to human health. Nevertheless, the Commission has always taken a precautionary approach and has acted on the assumption that the disease could be transmissible to humans.

In order to reduce as far as possible any risk of exposure to the BSE agent and to restore consumer confidence, the Commission took measures pending an overall examination of the situation, to ban the movement of live cattle and bovine products from the UK as soon as the British Government announced the possibility of a link between BSE and a new form of CJD. The Commission is also providing financial support to the UK to remove animals likely to have been exposed to contaminated feed from the food chain.

In addition, the Commission carries out missions of inspection to check on the correct application of the rules concerning the control of BSE.

Fats, tallow and gelatin from the UK were widely used by many sectors of the industry before the ban on their dispatch from the UK to other Member States and third countries.

Question No 85 by Caroline Jackson (H−0676/96)

Subject: Danger to consumers of under‐reporting of BSE cases

Is the Commission aware of the serious concerns expressed by the public and the Federation of Veterinarians in Europe that thousands of cases of BSE throughout the EU are not being reported? Does the Commission not think it surprising that so few cases have been reported outside the UK, given that many EU countries imported both animal feed and cattle in great numbers from the UK? Statistics suggest that of the 57 000 pure‐bred cattle exported from Britain between 1985 and 1990, the development of BSE would occur in some 1 670 cases. What is the Commission doing to ensure that all cases of BSE are recorded, and that the appropriate consumer safety measures are in place in all EU countries to deal with any possible link with CJD? Would the Commission not agree that the honest reporting of BSE cases and the strict controls put in place in the UK suggest that British beef is the safest in Europe?

Answer

The Commission is concerned to ensure that all beef on the market in the Community is safe to eat. BSE is therefore notifiable in all Member States, and the Commission has taken measures to ensure that each has the necessary expertise to carry out diagnosis. It is also a legal requirement that all animals are examined for signs of BSE before slaughter. Any that are suspicious must be detained pending investigation.

It is impossible to ensure that every farmer obeys the rules for notification of suspects, but this applies in all Member States in including the United Kingdom. Adequate financial compensation for the farmer is an important element in achieving full cooperation.

However, the under‐reporting suggested by the quoted data, if it is true, amounts to a tiny percentage of the cattle population in the Community compared with the numbers in the United Kingdom.

The matter has been referred to on several occasions by the Commission in the Standing Veterinary Committee. All Member States maintain that under‐reporting does not take place in their Member States and that effective surveillance is in place. The Commission will in the course of the second half of 1996 undertake inspections and will check that effective surveillance programmes are indeed in place, which will meet the objective of identifying suspect cases and carrying out proper investigations and diagnosis.

The Commission will not fail to report and act upon any findings which suggest under‐reporting.

***

Question No 86 by Anne McIntosh (H−0523/96)

Subject: Creutzfeldt‐Jacob disease

What action is the Commission proposing to take to control CJD in those countries where it already exists in serious proportions, yet where there is a low reported incidence of BSE?

Will not the Commission take measures to prevent the incidence of CJD, in particular by investigating its other potential causes, i.e. those other than BSE?

Would not the Commission agree that, sixty years since CJD was first recognized, we have not discovered its causes?

Answer

Creutzfeldt‐Jakob disease (CJD) is a rare disease. It is fortunate not having the terrible disease existing in any serious extent either in the Member States of the European Community or beyond it.

Information made available to the Commission from the Member States indicates that the incidence of CJD is between 0.5 and 1 case per one million inhabitants. From what has been reported from countries all over the world, there are no drastic departures from these figures.

Within the Community these figures have not changed since the beginning of the nineties, even if one includes the new variant of CJD. The Commission would remind the House that this new variant has now been confirmed in 13 cases in the UK and one in France.

Prevention of a disease can be achieved not only by understanding its causes, and identifying causative agents and modes of transmission, but also by devising appropriate preventative tools and methods, or deploying already existing means if their efficacy with relation to the disease has been demonstrated.

As regards CJD, causation has yet to be established, although a temporal association, as everybody knows, has been made with BSE in cattle. Modes of transmission, apart from the well‐established iatrogenic route, are the object of intense investigation.

The Community and the Member States, the UK in particular, have already taken a series of precautionary measures based on current scientific knowledge about this association.

Moreover, the Commission has been and is actively involved in developing research projects in order to improve epidemiological, clinical and basic knowledge about this disease, its causes and its transmission.

In particular, in line with the conclusions of the Council of Health Ministers in May 1996, the Commission is encouraging the extension of the epidemiological surveillance of CJD to all Member States using the same methods as have been applied already in five Member States.

Furthermore, the Commission has taken initiatives to collect and compile data from all Member States in order to obtain validated and comparable information on the pattern of the disease.

The House should rest assured that if causes and modes of transmission, or even associations of the disease with certain agents or factors are established, the Commission will make every effort towards determining and introducing preventative action.

Question No 87 by Doeke Eisma (H−0762/96)

Subject: FAO food summit

The world food conference is to take place in Rome from 14 to 17 November.

In what way will the Commission be participating in this conference; which directorates will be represented? What is the Commission's input to the conference? What does the Commission think about the disturbing reports that Japan will use this food summit to legitimise commercial whaling? In what way can the European Parliament participate in this conference?

Answer

In its capacity as a member of the FAO, the European Community has played an active part in every stage of the preparatory meetings and, especially, at the negotiations on the policy statement and the action plan. The active involvement of the Commission alongside the Member States has enabled the Community to influence the orientations of the Summit and to indicate its determination to promote viable recommendations to try to meet the challenge of a secure world food supply.

A communication by the Commission to the Council and the European Parliament, setting out the priorities with a view to the Summit, is now in preparation.

Furthermore, a financial contribution of ECU 400, 000 has been allocated to specific activities associated with the Summit, some of them for the benefit of the developing countries – an NGO forum and publications on a number of subjects of importance for the security of food supply.

During the negotiations preparatory to the Summit, the Commission has not been formally notified of – and has not even suspected – the fact that the Japanese authorities were hoping to use the Summit to legitimize commercial whaling. The Kyoto declaration on fisheries and food security, the code of conduct for responsible fishing and a number of other instruments which represent the most important references in this area already appear in the texts and are not disputed.

In accordance with what took place at previous United Nations conferences, and in compliance with interinstitutional practice and procedure, Members of the European Parliament who wish to do so may form part of the European Community delegation.

Moreover, the Geneva Inter‐Parliamentary Union, together with the Italian Parliament and in conjunction with the Summit Secretariat, is organizing an ‚MPs' Day at the World Food Summit’. This special meeting will be held at the Italian Senate in Rome on 15 November. According to information received, this meeting is open to Members of the European Parliament.

Question No 88 by Sören Wibe (H−0763/96)

Subject: The Phare programme in Albania

Owing to irregularities, the general elections held in Albania in May 1996 provoked almost unanimous international condemnation. The Council assured me on 21 June 1996, in response to a question, that respect for democratic principles and human rights and a constructive attitude on the part of the Albanian government were considered crucial for the further development of relations between the EU and Albania. The Albanian government has not, however, shown any signs of calling fresh elections and giving the opposition a fair chance.

What exactly has happened as regards the Phare programme in Albania? Has it continued as if nothing had changed, been suspended or been terminated?

Answer

Respect for human rights and democracy is the essential condition for the maintenance and development of relations between the Community and Albania.

The PHARE programme is one of the key elements in binding the countries of Central and Eastern Europe to democracy, providing their economies with the right conditions to bring about essential reforms. Such an aid programme can only be called into question in extreme cases. In any other situation, an excessively rigid attitude would be diametrically opposed to the intended objective and could only reduce the Community's freedom of action to influence the course of events and encourage the necessary changes. To date, the PHARE programme for Albania has not undergone any modification.

Through the PHARE programme, the European Commission financially supports the action by the Council of Europe aimed at local observation of the proper procedure at the forthcoming local elections on 20 October 1996. This particularly sensitive date will give Albania the opportunity to prove that aid provided earlier has borne fruit and that the country is continuing along the road towards democratization.

Question No 89 by Patrick Cox (H−0768/96)

Subject: Classification of goods

The EU is party to the internationally agreed system of classification of goods known as the Harmonised Commodity Description and Coding System (HS). The EU classified Local Area Network equipment to chapter 85 of the Combined Nomenclature (CN) in 1994/95 although other signatories to the HS (Japan, USA, Canada) continue to classify LAN equipment to chapter 84 of the CN. Can the Commission explain the basis for their decision?

The European Commission issued regulations 1638/94(10) and 1165/95(11) classifying specific Local Area Network equipment to chapter 85 in the CN. These regulations covered LAN adapter cards, link adapters, and transceivers. The EC has systematically classified ALL LAN equipment to chapter 85. Can the Commission explain why regulations 1638/94 and 1165/95 were extended to LAN equipment not specifically covered by these regulations?

Answer

As the honourable Member will be aware, one role of the Commission is to ensure the uniform application of Community legislation. The aim of classification regulations, such as the two Commission regulations referred to by the honourable Member, is to fulfil this obligation.

The two Commission regulations in question concern the classification of two types of adapter, a ‚network’ adapter card and a transceiver, in the combined nomenclature. These regulations were published in 1994 and 1995.

1. All of these items are used for the transmission of data, over a local area network (LAN), between computers. They are considered, by Member States' experts meeting within the framework of the Tariff and Statistical Section of the Customs Code Committee, as telecommunications apparatus, specifically covered by heading No 8517 of the Harmonized System (HS).

The classification was determined in accordance with the provisions of the section or chapter notes and the general rules for the interpretation of the combined nomenclature. The relevant provisions stipulate, in particular, that goods are to be classified in their specific headings, even if they are parts or accessories operating in conjunction with machines classified in other headings of the nomenclature.

The US, during 1990 and 1991, issued a number of rulings, classifying products falling within the category normally referred to as LAN equipment. These products were in the form of LAN‐interface boards.2 In September 1992, the US administration revoked these rulings, referring to, inter alia, ’non‐binding’ views of other customs administrations (not taking into consideration that the Community, in February 1992, had issued a regulation on another type of LAN equipment (multiplexing‐systems)).

2. It should be noted that LAN equipment covers a wide range of products from printed‐circuit boards to standalone equipment (like routers). Each product must be evaluated on its own merit. Of course, in the process of dayto‐day classification, considerations and conclusions, with regard to similar products previously classified, are taken into account by Member States (for example, whether they perform a specific function like transmission of data).

Apart from the above two regulations and the regulation concerning multiplexing‐systems (3), no other Commission regulation on such matters has been adopted since 23.5.95.

The Commission is fully aware of the problems related to the classification of IT/Multimedia products, where rapid technological changes are not easily manageable through existing classification rules. For that reason, the Commission is now examining the situation in consultation with industry in order to find an appropriate solution.

***

Question No 90 by John Cushnahan (H−0775/96)

Subject: Resources to combat drug trafficking

Is the Commission aware of the two recent major drug seizures along the Irish coast at Moneypoint (Co Clare) and Cork harbour?

As the Irish authorities have responsibility for policing over 20 % of European coastal waters, would the Commission be prepared to provide additional resources to the Irish Government for combatting drug trafficking?

Answer

Because the combating of trafficking in illicit drugs is a matter which falls within the Third Pillar of the Treaty on European Union and is the exclusive responsibility of the Member States enforcement services, the Commission does not keep records of such cases. However, it is aware of the two recent major drug seizures on the Irish coast from newspaper reports of them.

For the same reason, the question of funding to provide additional resources for the enforcement services is a matter for discussion between the Member States at Union level and any resources allocated for the purpose are dealt with under the provisions of Article K.8(2) of the Treaty on European Union.

For its own part and within the limits of its competence, the Commission is active in assisting the Customs Services of the Member States with the task of controlling the external frontier in any way that it can. In particular, it provides logistical support for Member States' joint surveillance operations (which cover First and Third Pillar matters, including drugs) and has also agreed to manage the Customs Information System under the terms of the 1995 Convention on the Use of Information Technology for Customs Purposes (OJ C316 of 27 November 1995), once that Convention comes into force.

Question No 91 by Eva Kjer Hansen (H−0776/96)

Subject: Wholesale infringements of EU directives by the Member States in invitations for tender in respect of public building and installation contracts

Following a major investigation, partly funded by the Commission, covering more than 30 000 public invitations to tender between 1990 and 1993, it has been shown that in 23.6 % of cases the time limits for tenders did not comply with EU rules. It was shown that the national authorities set shorter deadlines than required by the directives, thereby favouring local firms?.

In addition, more than 5 % of the awarding authorities stipulate such short time limits for obtaining the material necessary for the tender that firms outside the local area are unable to comply with them. In their current form the EU rules simply ignore this problem.

What initiatives does the Commission intend taking to prevail upon the authorities in the Member States to observe the rules?

What concrete legislative measures does the Commission intend to implement to ensure that firms can obtain the necessary material in time?

Does the Commission intend to initiate a follow‐up investigation for the period after 1993, and will this include other sectors and the three new Member States?

Answer

With the proviso that the Commission services have not finalized their analysis of the methodological reliability of the study in question, it is undeniable that there seems to be a general problem in relation to the conformity of the deadlines imposed by the contracting authorities in the tender notices. According to the study, the problem would manifest itself both in relation to the deadlines for the request of participation and/or the submission of tenders as well as the deadlines for the request of tender documents or supplementary information.

While the Commission will deal with the complaints it may receive regarding this issue, it is clear that it does not have the resources necessary to carry out a systematic and global control of the ever increasing number of notices published each year in the Official Journal of the European Communities – in 1995, over 95, 000 notices.

The Commission is therefore considering what other means can be employed to ensure better respect of the rules regarding deadlines, also bearing in mind the necessity of involving the Member States more closely in this effort. In this context, and as part of the general debate about to be launched as a preparation to the review of the application of the directives, provided for in most of them, the Commission services are preparing a Green Paper on public procurement, which could be issued shortly and which will also raise the problem of the deadlines. This should allow the Commission to obtain valuable contributions and perhaps good ideas as to possible solutions.

The Commission has also recognized that information technology can make a significant contribution to improve the notification and dissemination of tender notices.

To this end it has launched a pilot project in October 1995 called SIMAP (Système d'information pour les marchés publics – Information system for public procurement). This allows the on‐line transmission by computer of contract notices, contract award notices and PINs (prior information notices) to the Publications Office in Luxembourg before formal publication in the Official Journal.

With regard to the specific concerns of the honourable Member regarding notice deadlines, the SIMAP software has the advantage of checking notices generally and deadlines in particular, thus ensuring deadline compliance with the directives as well as more efficient transmission and qualitatively improved notices. At the same time the SIMAP system also allows contract notices to be published more rapidly thus allowing suppliers more time to respond.

The first phase of the pilot project has been successfully completed and is in use throughout the European Union. The Commission plans to increase the number of current users to around 1, 000 during the course of 1997 with the ultimate objective of generalizing the system so that it is widely used by the great number of contracting authorities.

In time the SIMAP system should help to alleviate some of the problems described by the honourable Member.

Joint Answer
