Question No 94 by Jörn Svensson (H−0780/96)

Subject: Protection of consumers against salmonella

Veterinary authorities in Sweden have noted an increase in the amount of salmonella‐infected meat entering the country. For Sweden, which prior to EU membership had effective safeguards against such infections, this could pose a serious health hazard. Inadequate border controls and unreliable health certificates from countries of export attesting to products' freedom from infection are putting Swedish consumers' health at risk.

What is the Commission's position regarding the possibility of introducing effective border controls for meat, for example on the basis of Articles 36 or 130r(1), second indent, of the Treaty?

Answer

In the context of the Treaty of accession of Austria, Finland and Sweden and following the approval of the Swedish national salmonella control programme, consignment of certain animals and animal products intended for Sweden are subject to additional guarantees in respect of salmonella. These guarantees cover breeding poultry, laying hens, table eggs, red meat, poultry meat and poultry for slaughter and are laid down in three Commission and three Council decisions. These decisions can be reviewed on the basis of reports prepared by Finland and Sweden in the light of the experience gained. The Commission has not been notified by the Swedish authorities that, due to an insufficiency of the additional guarantees provided for in the Community provisions, there has been a deterioration of the Swedish salmonella situation.

Question No 95 by Nikolaos Papakyriazis (H−0782/96)

Subject: Looting of ancient Greek monuments

In the former Yugoslav Republic of Skopje a gang of illicit dealers in antiquities has been arrested: they had in their possession a large number of ancient artefacts (some 2.000) dating from the 8th Century BC to the Byzantine period. The priceless cultural heritage of the ancient Greek province of Pelagonia has been constantly subject to looting for many years, and the Government of the FYROM has been powerless to do anything effective to combat this scourge.

What does the Commission intend to do to address this situation, in accordance with the international agreements on the protection and conservation of the cultural heritage and the restitution of stolen cultural goods?
More specifically, what measures does it intend to recommend that to the Government of Skopje take, within the framework of the future development of relations between this country and the European Union?
How will it ensure that the cultural heritage of the ancient Greek province of Pelagonia is effectively protected and that the monuments of European culture located there are preserved from further looting?

Answer

Pursuant to and within the limits of Article 36 of the EC Treaty, it is primarily a matter for each Member State to take steps to protect its national treasures possessing artistic, historic or archaeological value, and in particular action to counter unauthorized archaeological projects.

With a view to the completion of the single market, the Council adopted Regulation (EEC) No 3911/92 of 9 December 1992 on the exporting of cultural assets and Directive No 93/7/EEC of 15 March 1993 on the return of cultural assets unlawfully removed from the territory of a Member State. These instruments do not replace the national systems for protecting national treasures, but are additional to them. They offer supplementary protection, especially by setting up cooperation mechanisms.

Mutatis mutandis, these principles apply to third countries: primarily, it is a matter for each third country to take the necessary steps to safeguard its cultural heritage; additional protection may be instituted by way of international cooperation. In this context, it is appropriate to mention the Unidroit Convention of June 1995 on the international restitution of stolen or unlawfully exported cultural assets. This Convention, which has already been signed by several Member States and third countries but has not yet come into force, will make an important contribution to the protection of those national treasures which, together, make up the heritage of the human race.

Question No 96 by Carmen Fraga Estévez (H−0786/96)

Subject: Continued inclusion in food aid programmes of certain preserved products

The Community fish processing industry has recently expressed concern that the Commission may be considering removing mackerel and sardine preserves from one sector of the food aid programmes.

What are the Commission's plans in this area?

Answer

The European Commission is not intending to eliminate processed fish products from its food aid programmes. In fact, the new regulation on food aid and food security which came into force on 8 July 1996 refers to financing (inter alia) ‚... processing of agricultural and food products’ (Art. 5), together with the importance of helping the populations of developing countries to ‚obtain a balanced diet’ (Art. 1.3).

Processed fish products such as sardines as well as other canned fish have always constituted a minor component within the food aid package allocated by the Commission. Their use has been limited to beneficiaries such as particular groups of refugees in very specific cases (e.g. Palestinian refugees), in order to ensure diversified rations. Nonetheless, as is indicated in the new regulation, emphasis has been placed upon the supply of basic food products, such as cereals, vegetable oil and pulses.

Question No 97 by Freddy Blak (H−0790/96)

Subject: Information campaign for the enhancement and protection of food products

During the political negotiations that preceded the adoption of Council Regulation (EEC) 2081/92(12) , there was strong opposition to granting special financial aid to producers, producer groups, etc. engaged in producing, verifying and marketing products protected under this Council Regulation. This being so, does the Agriculture Commissioner consider it to be sound administrative practice for Community funds nevertheless to be used on an information campaign designed to help producers publicize the relevant products?

Answer

Article 5 of Commission Regulation (EEC) No 2037/93 lays down that ‚the Commission shall undertake such communication activities as are essential, excluding any aid to producers and/or processors, to make the public aware of the meaning of the indications ‚PDO’ and ‚PGI’, meaning protected designation of origin and protected geographical indication, in the Community languages’. The similar article is contained in Commission Regulation (EEC) No 1848/93 to ‚create awareness among the public of the meaning of the wording and of the community symbol’ in connection with certificates of identity. The Commission has therefore followed the decisions of the Council of Ministers, which had in fact deleted one article in the proposal, referring to the provision of financial aid to groups of producers.

The information campaign that has been instituted only explains to the public (producers, and also distributers and consumers) the concepts and the complex procedures established at community level. Recourse to these Community systems being voluntary rather than obligatory, it is indeed necessary for this Community Regulation to be fully understood in order to be fully effective. The producer needs to understand how to gain access to the Community Regulation, and the distributor and consumer need to understand the meeting of the wording and logos which they will see on product labels.

Question No 98 by Niall Andrews (H−0794/96)

Subject: City centre renewal

With a view to encouraging the revitalization of city centres, many of which are suffering from de‐population, low‐quality development and crime, will the Commission consider the establishment of an annual award whereby the most imaginative schemes put forward by city councils and corporations for revitalizing and improving the physical environment of their city centres are co‐financed from the EU budget?

Answer

The Commission attaches great importance to the revitalization of city centres. Under its current Urban Pilot Projects, one of the four priorities for action is the revitalization of historic centres. In the recent call for proposals for Urban Pilot Projects 1995‐1999 this has been extended to cover a wider range of city centre problems. As the projects are funded under Article 10 of the European Regional Development Fund regulation(13) , a key criterion for selection will be an emphasis on innovative and imaginative solutions put forward by urban, local and regional authorities.

In respect of the possibility of an aware, the Commission has supported two European Urban and Regional Planning Awards schemes to draw attention to planning projects of the greatest merit and to encourage dissemination of best practice across Europe. Moreover, on 8 October 1996 an award was given to 5 municipalities within the Sustainable Cities and Towns Campaign supported by the Commission.

The Commission is giving consideration to launching a further urban and regional planning award scheme in the future.

Question No 99 by James Fitzsimons (H−0800/96)

Subject: Urban renewal

In the context of the forthcoming review of the operation of the Structural Funds after 1999, has the Commission examined the options which exist for diverting an increased amount of the resources of these funds into promoting environmental improvement and urban renewal projects in small and medium‐sized urban areas and will the Commission bring forward proposals which are specifically aimed at tackling the problems of these types of areas?

Answer

The Commission has been giving increasing attention to urban projects and has put a number of actions into operation. These include the Urban Pilot Projects programmes launched from 1989‐1993 with a budget of around 100 MECU, as well as the URBAN Community Initiative, whose budget was increased earlier this year to a total of over 870 MECU up to 1999. A further 60 MECU will be allocated(14) to cofinance around 25 new Urban Pilot Projects 1995‐1999, for which the selection procedure is in progress. The massive response – over 500 cities and urban areas responded to the recent call for proposals for Urban Pilot Projects – is a further indication of the need for action on urban issues at Community level.

In addition to these specific programmes there are many actions located in urban areas and cofinanced in operational programmes within the main Community Support Frameworks in Member States.

The Commission recognizes that some of the Community's most serious socio‐economic and environmental problems occur within cities. It is clear that the role of small and medium‐sized urban areas will be an element on the agenda in discussions regarding the future of the Structural Funds.

Question No 100 by Gerard Collins (H−0796/96)

Subject: Unused Structural Fund aid

In view of the reports that approximately 20 billion ECU of Structural Fund aid allocated by the Commission over the last ten years has still not been utilized by the proposers of approved projects, does the Commission have any plans to direct unused resources to projects which are ready to proceed, even if they are located in other Member States?

Answer

It is quite normal that, at any given time, part of the financial commitments made by the Commission in respect of structural fund programmes should not yet have given rise to specific financial transfers to the Member States. The level of these ‚outstanding commitments’ has indeed risen over the past two years, but that is linked to the increase in the overall volume of credits devoted to the structural funds; the ratio of outstanding commitments to annual commitment appropriations has in fact declined since 1993, implying an improved execution level for the funds.

The question of redirecting resources does not arise. Within the overall budgetary limits for any given year, the Commission makes payments to each Member State as and when the payments are requested as a result of the progress on the ground of programmes previously approved.

Question No 101 by Brian Crowley (H−0798/96)

Subject: Payment of ESF grants to students

Is the Commission aware of the considerable disparities which exist between Member States with regard to the interpretation of the rules governing the payment of ESF grants to students, citizens of a EU country who are not nationals of the country in which they are pursuing a training course but are EU citizens? Is so, will the Commission now take steps to ensure that the rules governing the payment of ESF grants are applied in an even‐handed manner in each of the Member States?

Answer

Article 2 of Regulation (EEC) No 2084/93 provides that the ESF may contribute to, inter alia, the payments made to persons participating in eligible activities.

Under these conditions, it is a matter for the Member States to define the practical arrangements for granting or not granting such payments, in compliance with the European rules on equality of treatment.

The Commission is not presently aware of cases of discrimination between trainees regarding the allocation of payments, grants or equivalent expenditures. It requests the honourable Member to supply it with the information needed to enable it to take the appropriate steps immediately.

Question No 102 by Liam Hyland (H−0804/96)

Subject: Reform of the European Union milk sector

The Commission is reported to be planning to table proposals for reforming the European Union milk sector early next year. Does it remain committed to the milk quota system for individual farmers and will the continuation of the milk quota system form an integral part of the proposals to be tabled by the Commission next year?

Answer

The current quota regime expires in 1999/2000. The Community is facing a fundamental choice for the dairy sector in either moving to a more market oriented system, eventually allowing a progressive relaxation of quotas in the longer run, or sticking to price support and a form of supply management.

The former would be in line with the general orientation favoured by the Commission in the strategy paper. It however raises the question of how to let the sector adjust to a structurally much lower price level and how to finance the adjustment. Ideas which have been floated so far range from introducing a headage system similar to the beef regime (a dairy cow premium) to area payments (a grass or forage premium) encompassing the whole ruminant livestock sector (dairy, beef, sheep).

The latter would imply adjusting the quota system to further WTO constraints through volume and most likely also price cuts as subsidized export possibilities and tariffs are reduced. A variant, avoiding a volume cut but increasing the complexity of the administration of quotas, could be a double price, double quota system, if it could be designed in such a way as to be WTO compliant. This, however, would not appear to be certain.

The Commission is currently examining the different policy options and the Commission will most likely present its views on the future of the dairy regime next spring.

***

Question No 103 by John McCartin (H−0808/96)

Subject: Common market organization in potatoes

Is the Commission aware of the six‐point plan for a common market organization in the potato sector and has it made or does it intend to make any proposals to establish a common market organization to improve the market organization of the potato crop?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the six point plan elaborated by COPA/COGECA.

In 1992, the Commission presented to the Council a proposal for a common organization potatoes.

Such a regime would ensure fair terms of competition and, through its provisions on marketing standards help to improve the transparency and efficiency of the market.

There would be real gains, and it is difficult to see now either those Member States who want more or those who want much less would lose anything from giving such a regime a fair trial.

The Commission remains convinced that the existing proposal represents the best compromise between those Member States wishing to see a rather heavy regime and those who want no regime at all.

Question No 104 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0813/96)

Subject: Implementation of the operational programme for the tourist industry

The operational programme for the tourist industry, forming part of the CSF, was adopted by Commission decision of 30 November 1995 for the period 22 November 1994 to 31 December 1999. In the light of the problems affecting the tourist industry in Greece, what is the take‐up rate for this programme to date? Has it been decided which marinas are to be funded and what procedure will be followed in regard to private sector involvement in their construction and operation? Have the locations been selected for the creation of integrated tourist development regions and what are they?

Answer

The Tourism and Culture operational programme envisages, for the years 1995 and 1996, total commitments by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) of MECU 73.6, including MECU 27.1 for the Tourism subprogramme (SP).

At the present time, MECU 19.1 has been committed (including MECU 1.4 for the Tourism SP); commitment of the remaining MECU 54.5 (including MECU 25.7 for the Tourism SP) has been applied for and will take place shortly.

The payments already made under the auspices of the ERDF amount to MECU 9.5 (including MECU 0.7 for the Tourism SP); a request for payment of MECU 5.7 (including MECU 1.7 for the Tourism SP) is currently being approved; a further request for payment of MECU 27.2 (including MECU 12.8 for the Tourism SP) is expected.

Public spending on the ground currently amounts to MECU 16.2 (including MECU 4.04 for the Tourism SP).

As regards the marinas and the integrated tourist development areas, the Commission expects to receive the final decisions and plans of the national authorities very shortly.

Question No 106 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0820/96)

Subject: Supplies to the Canary Islands

Does the Commission consider that the present method of determining aid levels for supplies to the Canary Islands, based on export refunds and the differences between Community and world market prices, is the most suitable in the light of the aims and objectives of the specific supply regime for the region?

Answer

Council Regulation (EEC) No 1601/92(15) , on measures relating to certain agricultural products for the benefit of the Canary Islands, lays down in Article 3(II) that ‚the Canary Islands shall likewise be supplied by the provision of community products held in public stocks, by way of intervention measures, or available on the Community market on terms equivalent, for the end user, to the advantage deriving from exemption from import duties for products originating in third countries’.

Given the current relationship between Community prices and world prices, the Commission is currently considering the matter in order to find an appropriate technical solution.

Question No 107 by Lone Dybkjær (H−0824/96)

Subject: Commission initiatives on Community staffing policy

Will the Commission explain who is responsible for laying down Community staffing policy and what ideas it has had regarding such policy in the light of information technology, public service considerations and future enlargement? What specific initiatives is it currently considering?

Answer

The power of decision is exercised by the Commission collectively in all areas. The Commissioner responsible for these matters, Mr Liikanen, is for his part responsible for preparing and implementing the Commission's decisions regarding the definition of the personnel policy (with the exception of research personnel and delegations outside the Community). For this purpose, he has a supervisory power over the staff of the Directorate‐General for Personnel and Administration.

As the honourable Member is aware, the Commission is presently working to improve its internal administration, a substantial proportion of which is represented by the personnel policy (Sound and efficient management 2000). In order to comply with a recommendation by Parliament's Committee on Budgets, it has also initiated regular and targeted structural rationalization schemes in certain Directorates‐General, in order regularly to verify proper consistency between its political priorities and the allocation of human resources. The European Parliament will be kept duly informed of the progress of these regular activities, the first of which are being undertaken this year.

The forward‐looking aspect of the personnel policy is also being taken into account by an informal working group recently set up by Mr Liikanen. It has just embarked on initial discussions on the possible changes in the institution between now and the year 2010, especially in the light of possible changes in the political environment, such as the results of the Intergovernmental Conference or the forthcoming enlargements of the Union.

These discussions, which are not at present restricted by any immediate operational objective, will eventually result in new guidelines on personnel, which guidelines will be notified to Parliament on the occasion of future budgetary procedures.

Question No 108 by Lyndon Harrison (H−0826/96)

Subject: Effects of the single market on children and young people

The Treaty on European Union recognizes the potentially negative effect of the single market in many areas such as social, environment and health and safety policy. Yet in no place in the Treaty is it even accepted that the single market will have an effect on children and young people in the European Union.

Does the Commission agree that children have been affected in specific ways by the single market, and that they may suffer more severely than adults from effects such as movement of families across borders and more freedom of movement for paedophiles?

Does the Commission therefore agree that this effect of the single market should be recognized in the Treaty by a specific reference to children and young people?

Answer

As any other citizen of the Union, children and young people are directly affected by the single market. For instance, they are consumers and are therefore concerned by the Community's consumer policy or indeed by the social security provisions when they moved from one Member State to another as beneficiaries of services. More generally they benefit from free movement of people provisions, in particular in relation to family rights. A wide range of Community legislation has also been set up specifically in order to protect children, such as the toy Directive, or to allow the young to benefit from the single market, for example the provisions giving the right for young people to study in another Member State and the setting up of specific exchange programmes, such as Erasmus, co‐financed by Community funds.

The intense work going on in the Third Pillar is paving the way for an area without frontiers which is both an area of freedom and security for the citizens of Europe. Within the Third Pillar, the Commission is especially addressing questions that have an impact on movement of persons, such as matters concerning family law, including custody for children, and ways to solve legal disputes between persons in different Member States.

Question No 109 by Susan Waddington (H−0831/96)

Subject: Child abusers' register

Parliamentarians and EU justice ministers have in the last month called for the strengthening of Europol in its capacity to collect information and data on those who have been convicted of crimes against children.

What is the current situation with regard to national registers and what proposals does the Commission have for the establishment of an EU‐wide register of those convicted of such crimes, and the implementation and use of such a register?

Answer

1. The ‚Justice and Internal Affairs’ Council, at its informal meeting on 27 September 1996, accepted the principle of extending the ‚drugs’ unit of Europol to include a campaign against trafficking in human beings and the sexual exploitation of children, together with the establishment of a programme including, among other things, a study of the appropriateness and feasibility of an exchange of information on missing children.

2. The Commission has no information on the existence or content of national registers of persons convicted of sexual abuse.

Question No 110 by Glenys Kinnock (H−0846/96)

Subject: Commission policies for children

The recent horrific cases of child abuse in Belgium have concentrated minds in the Commission, Council and Parliament on dealing with specific concerns of identifiable groups of children in terms of sexual exploitation, child prostitution, trafficking of human beings and child pornography.

Does the Commission agree that what is lacking is a coherent policy and strategy which responds to the needs and interests of all children and young people in the European Union?

Answer

The Commission shares the opinion of the honourable parliamentarian concerning the need for reinforcement at Union level in the cooperation and coordination of the fight against trafficking and exploitation of children.

In the light of the resolution on young victims of violence adopted by the European Parliament during its session in September 1996, the Commission has already presented to the informal Justice and Home Affairs Council of 27 September 1996 its initial reactions on the means of implementing at a Union level, the programme of actions adopted by the World Congress held in Stockholm in August 1996. During this meeting a political agreement was reached on three joint actions:

‐ an extension of the Europol Drug Unit mandate from illegal migrants to trafficking in human beings, this including trafficking in children;

‐ an incentive and exchange programme for persons responsible for combating trade in human beings and sexual exploitation of children;

‐ the establishment of a directory of centres of excellence in this matter.

The Commission has a coherent policy concerning the fight against sexual exploitation of children. The Commissioner responsible for the coordination is Anita Gradin. Within the remit of this coordinated policy, the Commission adopted on 16 October a Communication to the Council and Parliament on illegal use of Internet, as well as a Green Paper on The Protection of Minors and Human Dignity in Audiovisual and Information Services. Further initiatives are planned.

Question No 111 by Antoinette Spaak (H−0828/96)

Subject: Moonlighting at the Commission

There have recently been reports in the Belgian press of moonlighting at the Commission.

Ruses of this kind could naturally not be tolerated, since they harm the interests both of the State, to the extent that State social legislation is violated, and of the workers concerned, who are denied all form of protection.

Can the Commission say whether the reports are based merely on rumours? Alternatively, if the above practices did take place, have appropriate penalties been imposed in order to stamp out the abuse?

Answer

The Commission wishes to inform the honourable Member that there is no question of undeclared working within the Commission, and more particularly in the case mentioned, the person concerned having been engaged by the Commission as a provider of services in return for payment of fees.

The question of principle relating to the classification of the working relationship between the Commission and the person concerned is currently the subject of proceedings before the Belgian courts, which have jurisdiction in this matter.

In the present situation, the Commission must refrain from comment pending a decision by the Court.

Question No 112 by Tommy Waidelich (H−0830/96)

Subject: Financing of trans‐European networks

Completion of the trans‐European networks is a priority for the EU. Their significance was not only stressed in the Commission White Paper on growth, competitiveness and employment, but has also been confirmed at several meetings of the European Council. Financial problems exist, however, so that new financing arrangements must be looked into as soon as possible.

What view does the Commission take of alternative methods of financing which promote joint ventures between the public and private sectors, e.g. the issue of ‚Eurobonds’ or other financial instruments which guarantee a return, and what is required to establish a financing system of that type?

Answer

Serious financing problems for TEN projects are mainly restricted to the transport networks. Some kinds of transport projects may become self‐sustainable thanks to the fact that revenues are generated (e.g. in the form of tolls). However, many of the priority projects selected by the European Council at Essen have low financial profitabilities and involve large risks. To raise these profitabilities to the point where projects become financially viable a volume of grants is needed, be they of local, national, or Community origin. Asset transfers may also fulfil this role. Loans or bonds can only contribute to make financing cheaper to the extent that the EIB or the European Community can borrow at the lowest rates available because of their optimal rating.

Even with grant support risk remains. The European Investment Fund provides long‐term guarantees to cope with it. The Fund has also been recently allowed to provide equity which is another instrument that absorbs risk.

A large part of TEN financing, whether public or private, will take the form of loans, or to a lesser extent bonds. The EIB can provide large loans at long maturities and very favourable terms to any financial package where the basic requirements of profitability and risk coverage have been solved.

The Commission has considered different forms of complementing a TEN project financial structure. Some like the ‚Union Bonds’ or the ‚topping‐up’ of the TEN budget line have so far been rejected by the Ecofin Council, others like the EIF have been accepted. Also the EIB with its ‚TEN window’ tries to better adapt its loans to the needs of TEN projects.

Question No 113 by Edward Newman (H−0833/96)

Subject: Children and the Commission

How does the Commission ensure the proper surveillance of issues that may affect children and young people as they arise in different Directorates‐General in bringing forward legislation and programmes?

Answer

There does not exist within the Commission a single unit responsible for all questions relating to children. The work of different units in the Commission are, however, fully coordinated. Furthermore, the Commission is developing a coherent policy concerning the fight against sexual exploitation of children.

Within the remit of this coordinated policy, Commissioner Anita Gradin presented an aide‐mémoire on the followup of the Stockholm conference to the informal Justice and Home Affairs Council of 27 September. The Commission adopted on 16 October a Communication to Council and Parliament on illegal use of Internet, as well as a Green Paper on the Protection of Minors and Human Dignity in Audiovisual and Information Services. Furthermore, within the framework of the Fourth Action Opportunities for Women and Men (1996‐2000) the Commission is committed to the continuation of work and research in the area of reconciliation of work and family life, a significant part of which relates to childcare services. The Commission has supported research and other projects on the quality and quantity of childcare in Member States. It is currently drawing up a report on the implementation of the Council Recommendation on Childcare (1992), prior to examining what measures may be necessary to promote higher standards for the care of children. Further initiatives are planned.

Question No 114 by David Morris (H−0834/96)

Subject: Protection of animals at time of slaughter

In Article 13(2)(c) of Directive 93/119/EC(16) on the protection of animals at the time of slaughter or killing, the Commission is required, by 31 December 1995, to submit a report to the Standing Veterinary Committee together with appropriate proposals for improving the rules on electrical and gaseous pre‐slaughter stunning of animals. Has the Commission submitted such a report and, if not, why not?

Answer

Although good progress has been made, the Scientific Veterinary Committee has not yet been able to give its opinion on detailed requirements for the slaughter and killing of animals.

The Directive requires the Scientific Veterinary Committee to give an opinion on:

‐ free bullet pistols, applied to the brain,

‐ the use of gases or combination of gases for stunning, and more in particular carbon dioxide for stunning poultry,

‐ the use of gases or combination of gases for killing,

‐ the use of the current necessary to stun the various species,

‐ any other scientific procedure for stunning or killing.

This has taken longer than expected but the Commission expects that the Scientific Committee will have the report ready soon.

Question No 115 by Gary Titley (H−0836/96)

Subject: Commission proposals for anti‐dumping measures against raw textile material imports

Will the Commission assure the many concerned companies in my constituency that any proposed anti‐dumping measures aimed at the import of raw textile material into the EU will not be introduced if these measures would have an adverse effect on businesses and jobs in the clothing, textile and fabric sector?

Answer

The Commission can assure the honourable Member that before deciding on the necessity, or otherwise, for any anti‐dumping measures, the Community interest as a whole, including the interests of its domestic industry, users and consumers will be carefully considered. The legal framework for anti‐dumping, i.e. the Basic Anti‐Dumping Regulation, gives all interested parties the opportunity to come forward and express their views.

Question No 116 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−0841/96)

Subject: Irregularities in the operation of vocational training centres in Greece

According to a letter from the Commission to the Greek Ministry of Employment, major problems and irregularities are continuing to affect the operation of vocational training centres and in particular the National Certification Centre in Greece. Significantly, the letter states that no measures have been taken concerning irregularities already ascertained, that there was lack of transparency in the recruitment of staff prior to the elections and that, in general, no progress has been made in improving working methods and fundamentally rationalizing the Centre. For this reason, calls are being made for the immediate suspension of its activities. Similarly, funding for the national programme for continuing education may also be halted unless certain conditions are met.

In view of the fact that, in the past, major irregularities have been uncovered concerning the operation and, in particular, the certification of vocational training centres in Greece, what progress has been made in deliberations between the Commission and the Greek Government and what precise measures will the Commission take with a view to solving permanently this problem which, in the final analysis, is depriving both the unemployed and the employed in Greece of funding and opportunities?

Answer

The European Commission, following the initial checks undertaken in 1995 and the discovery of the irregularities, requested the competent authorities in the Member State to remedy the situation at the National Certification Centre.

It notified the competent authorities that, before the start of the 1996 programmes, they would have to provide all necessary guarantees that the Centre was functioning transparently and effectively.

Despite the assertions of the Greek authorities, the inspections of July 1996 have demonstrated that no progress has been made with regard to the functioning of the Centre.

In view of the gravity of the situation, the European Commission has been compelled to ask that certification work at the Centre should cease immediately until an effective solution to this problem is in place.

The European Commission wishes to remind the honourable Member that the reform of the National Certification Centre is within the jurisdiction of the Member State. In this situation, it has requested the Greek authorities to submit a plan for the total reconstruction of the Centre.

The European Commission, being aware of the problems caused by the delay in the implementation of the programmes to assist the unemployed and those in work, is prepared to implement transitional arrangements, in conjunction with the Greek authorities, pending the introduction of credible certification procedures.

Question No 117 by Alan Gillis (H−0845/96)

Subject: Beef export markets

Given the present crisis in the beef industry in the European Union, what concrete measures is the Commission taking to reopen beef export markets and renegotiate WTO export rules?

Answer

At the end of April this year the beef export refund level was increased by 10‐12 % in order to support the exports of beef. At present the weekly applications for export licences are at a very satisfactory level.

The Commission is doing everything it can, in collaboration with the Member States, to ensure that traditional markets for cattle and beef remain open or are reopened. At this stage, it is premature to conclude that it would be desirable to renegotiate WTO export rules, particularly because it would be implausible to obtain a result exclusively for beef.

Question No 118 by Charlotte Cederschiöld (H−0847/96)

Subject: The Commission's plans for anti‐drug programmes

Mrs Bonino, the Commissioner responsible for fisheries policy, said in Paris on 9 October that certain narcotic preparations should be legalized. She considered Dutch legislation to have achieved tangible results in the form of fewer crimes and fewer people infected with aids.

In view of Mrs Bonino's remarks, who, the Community or Member States, does the Commission believe should be responsible for the treatment of drug addicts?

Answer

The European Commission has no legal power in relation to penal legislation. Criminal sanctions for drugs‐related offences is therefore a matter for Member States' policy.

The Member States are responsible for the treatment of drug addicts. The Community can, however, lend its support to Member States' action aiming to contributing to a high level of human health protection.

Question No 119 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0664/96)

Subject: Access charges

Under Directive 90/387/EEC(17) mobile telephone company tariffs for access to the public telecommunications network must be cost‐oriented. However, this is not being done in Greece, where a study of tariff structures commissioned by the European Union has revealed that access charges are not cost‐oriented, as they are in other Member States. In Greece, the network access charge is Dr 6.5 per minute.

What are the access charges per minute in other Member States?

Is the Commission considering the possibility of initiating proceedings against Greece if it emerges that access charges in Greece are below cost levels?

Answer

1. The interconnection agreements (including the determination of costs) are the subject of commercial negotiations between the parties concerned. Since these agreements are legal transactions concluded between private enterprises, the specific conditions incorporated therein remain confidential in some Member States.

The Commission has no comparative information on prices for interconnection between mobile network operators and fixed network operators in the Member States of the Union.

2. In the absence of Community legislation regulating interconnection problems (the draft Directive has not yet been adopted by the EP or the Council), the Commission is not contemplating any specific action against Greece in this matter.

Question No 120 by John Iversen (H−0680/96)

Subject: Exemption of ferries from fuel tax

Is the Commission prepared, for environmental reasons and on account of noise pollution, to impose fuel tax on high‐speed ferries such as the catamarans which operate between Århus and Kalundborg?

Answer

Article 8(1)(c) of Directive 92/81/EEC requires the Member States to exempt from the harmonized excise duty those mineral oils which are used as fuel for commercial navigation in Community waters. Furthermore, Article 3 (2) of Directive 92/12/EEC allows the Member States to impose upon these same products other, nonharmonized forms of national indirect taxation, on condition that these comply with the fiscal regulations on mineral oils laid down at Community level.

On the basis of the abovementioned provisions, the consumption of oil as a fuel in commercial navigation between Aarhus and Kalundborg has been exempted from the harmonized excise duty and, consequently, is also exempt from the specific tax which the Danish authorities have imposed for environmental reasons.
